In this first course, we will cover the foundational techniques for NLP analysis that serve as a solid basis for understanding modern techniques that will be covered in the future, two subsequent courses.  However, this course is offered as a standalone couse, because it is a meaningful unit to introduce key concepts in natural language processing; and second, to get you started in an easy, digestible format well-suited to casual, self-paced learning.

In Section 1, we give a bird's eye Overview of the landscape that is NLP.  We describe the root problem of NLP â ambiguity â and how it manifests at every level of linguistic interest to NLP.  
In Section 2, we delve into the basic unit of language, Words, and examine how to process them through regular expressions and determine their similarity through minimum edit distance. ChatGPTSection 3: Syntax and Structure of Sentences
This section introduces the basic syntactic structures in language. We explore how sentences are formed, the hierarchical organization of phrases, and the relationships between words. Parsing techniques like context-free grammars (CFG) and dependency parsing are discussed. This provides a foundation for understanding how machines can derive meaning from the structure of text.Section 4: Morphological Analysis
Here, we break down words into their smallest meaningful units, known as morphemes. We discuss stemming, which reduces words to their base or root form, and morphological parsing, which identifies the structure of complex words. This analysis helps in understanding the nuances of word formation and its implications in NLP.Section 5: Part-of-Speech Tagging
Every word in a sentence has a role. In this section, we learn about labeling words with their corresponding part-of-speech (POS). This helps in identifying the syntactic and semantic role of words in a sentence. Techniques like hidden Markov models (HMM) and rule-based approaches for POS tagging are covered.Section 6: N-grams and Language Modeling
Language modeling is crucial for predicting the probability of word sequences. We dive deep into n-grams, sequences of 'n' words used for prediction, and how these models can predict the likelihood of a word sequence. This section covers the concepts of conditional probability and smoothing techniques, setting the stage for more advanced probabilistic models in subsequent courses.Section 7: Summary and Next Steps
Concluding this foundational course, we recap the key concepts learned and provide a brief glimpse into what's next in the world of NLP. We emphasize the importance of the basics, as they lay the groundwork for understanding advanced techniques and the ever-evolving field of natural language processing.Course Features:Interactive Exercises: Throughout the course, engage with hands-on exercises to reinforce your understanding of the concepts.Case Studies: Real-world examples that demonstrate the practical application of NLP techniques.Discussion Forums: Engage with peers and instructors, share insights, and seek clarifications on challenging topics.Quizzes and Assessments: Test your knowledge and understanding of the material with regular quizzes and a final assessment.By the end of this course, you will have a strong foundation in the basics of NLP. This knowledge will not only serve as a stepping stone for advanced topics but will also empower you to harness the potential of language processing in various real-world applications.Section 3 covers basic N-gram Language Models, which serve as the foundation for how current NLP architectures represent sequences of words. Reflect on the foundational NLP techniques and their significance in real-world applications.Further Resources and Reading: A curated list of resources, books, and courses for those eager to delve deeper into NLP.Feedback and Course Evaluation: Share your experiences, suggestions, and feedback to help us improve the course for future learners.Upon completion of this course, learners will have a firm grasp of foundational NLP concepts and techniques. Not only will you be equipped with knowledge, but you'll also have hands-on experience through the assignments, giving you confidence to explore more advanced topics in NLP or apply these techniques in your projects.As many of you noticed, the phrase Natural Language Processing, is a bit ambiguous.  But why?

Let's center on the middle word, Language.  Is it more closely connected with its left neighbour, Natural, or its right neighbour, Processing?

It turns out that because human language (that is, natural language) is ambiguous, processing language for computers is difficult.  But we're getting ahead of ourselves â more on that later!

For now let's say that in English, Language associates with its left neighbour more closely.  Then we are talking about the processing of natural languages, such as English, Mandarin Chinese, and Bahasa Indonesia, for example.

When we interact with a computer system using natural language, the computer must build a representation of what we say.  Let's call this representation R.

The process of making sense of either the input text or speech of a person to derive its representation R, we term  Analysis.  But what good is a representation by itself?  A computer needs to react to R in some way to let us know it understands what to do with our input.  One way of doing this is to respond using natural language back to us; again, either in text or speech form.  After reasoning on R and building a response representation R', it could respond back in natural language in an inverse process.  This process is called Generation.  

NL Analysis and NL Generation form a reciprocal functional pair: NLG(NLA(natural language text) = natural language text.  That is, going in a loop of analysis followed by generation for the same NL text should produce a text with similar meaning to the original input.  

Note that Generation is only one way that a computer can respond; there are many other ways for a computer to respond, including executing actions.  ChatGPTNatural Language Understanding and Action ExecutionBeyond just generating a response, computers can also act upon the representation, R. This aspect of processing is often termed as Natural Language Understanding (NLU). It's the step that bridges the gap between raw representation and actionable intelligence.For instance, consider a voice assistant like Alexa or Siri. When you say, "Set an alarm for 7 AM," the system first processes your speech into a text representation, then it analyzes this to understand your intent, which in this case is to set an alarm. The action that follows â the actual setting of the alarm â is based on the computer's understanding of your request.Challenges in NLPOne of the main challenges in NLP is ambiguity. This can manifest in several ways:Lexical Ambiguity: A single word can have multiple meanings. For example, the word "bank" can refer to a financial institution or the side of a river.Syntactic Ambiguity: A sentence can be interpreted in multiple ways due to its structure. For instance, "I saw the man with the telescope" can be understood as either you using the telescope to see the man or you seeing a man who has a telescope.Pragmatic Ambiguity: Sometimes, the context in which something is said can change its meaning. For instance, the phrase "Can you pass the salt?" is technically a question about one's ability, but pragmatically it's a request.Achieving Fluent InteractionFor a computer system to truly understand and generate natural language, it must overcome these challenges of ambiguity. It must not only decode the literal meaning of words and phrases but also infer the intended meaning based on context, previous interactions, and even cultural nuances.Additionally, while generation of a response is one approach, more sophisticated systems will also integrate other forms of response, whether it's carrying out an action, pulling up relevant data, or even asking clarifying questions to resolve ambiguity.Closing ThoughtsNatural Language Processing, at its core, is about bridging the gap between human communication and machine understanding. It's an ever-evolving field, with new challenges emerging as we uncover deeper intricacies of human language and thought. But as technology continues to advance, the potential for more intuitive, natural interactions with our machines becomes increasingly within reach.NLP in One Slide

Lexical Analysis:Deals with words' structure and meaning.Processes include Tokenization, Normalization, Stemming, and Lemmatization.2. Syntactic Analysis:Focuses on the organization of words in sentences.Involves Part-of-Speech Tagging and Syntactic Parsing.3. I would probably be using vim or grepChatGPTOf course! Regular expressions (often abbreviated as "regex" or "regexp") are powerful tools for matching strings based on patterns. They are widely used in text processing and searching. Here's a brief introduction to regex, tailored for usage with tools like vim and grep:Basic Metacharacters:.: Matches any single character (except newline).^: Matches the start of a line.$: Matches the end of a line.*: Matches zero or more of the preceding element.+: Matches one or more of the preceding element.?: Matches zero or one of the preceding element.Character Classes:[...]: Matches any one character inside the brackets.Example: [aeiou] matches any vowel.[^...]: Matches any one character NOT inside the brackets.Example: [^0-9] matches any non-digit.\d: Matches any digit, equivalent to [0-9].\D: Matches any non-digit.\w: Matches any word character (alphanumeric + underscore).\W: Matches any non-word character.\s: Matches any whitespace character (spaces, tabs, line breaks).\S: Matches any non-whitespace character.Quantifiers:{n}: Matches exactly n occurrences of the preceding element.{n,}: Matches n or more occurrences.{n,m}: Matches between n and m occurrences.Special Sequences:\b: Matches the empty string, but only at the beginning or end of a word.\B: Matches the empty string, but NOT at the beginning or end of a word.Grouping and Alternation:( ...): Groups several characters together.|: Acts like an OR operator.Example: apple|banana matches either "apple" or "banana".Escape Sequences:If you want to match a metacharacter like ., ^, $, etc., you need to escape it with a backslash \. For instance, to match a literal dot, use \..Usage in vim:In vim, to search using regex, you'd typically start with / followed by your pattern. Example: /apple|banana to find either "apple" or "banana".Usage in grep:With grep, you'd use a command like:perlCopy codegrep 'apple|banana' filename.txt
For extended regular expressions (which might be needed for some of the above patterns like |), you'd use grep with the -E flag:perlCopy codegrep -E 'apple|banana' filename.txt
This is a basic overview. Regular expressions can get very complex and are a powerful tool for text searching and manipulation. With practice, you'll be able to craft intricate patterns to match exactly what you're looking for!Give some examples of how to use the find command in bashChatGPTCertainly! The find command in Bash is a powerful tool for searching files and directories. Here are some common usage examples:Find Files by NameFind all files named file.txt under the current directory:bashCopy codefind . -name file.txt
Case-insensitive search:bashCopy codefind . -iname file.txt
Find Directories by NameFind all directories named docs:bashCopy codefind . -type d -name docs
Find Based on File TypeFind all directories:bashCopy codefind . -type d
Find all symbolic links:bashCopy codefind . -type l
Find Files with Specific PermissionsFind files with 755 permissions:bashCopy codefind . -type f -perm 755
Find Files by SizeFind files larger than 50MB:bashCopy codefind . -type f -size +50M
Find files smaller than 50MB:bashCopy codefind . -type f -size -50M
Find and Remove FilesFind and remove all .tmp files:bashCopy codefind . -name '*.tmp' -exec rm {} \;
Find Files Modified within the Last DayFind files modified within the last 24 hours:bashCopy codefind . -mtime -1
Limit Depth of SearchSearch only in the current directory (don't descend into subdirectories):bashCopy codefind . -maxdepth 1 -name file.txt
Find by OwnerFind all files owned by user john:bashCopy codefind . -owner john
Using Multiple CriteriaFind all .txt files that were modified within the last 24 hours:bashCopy codefind . -name '*.txt' -mtime -1
Remember, find is a versatile command, and there are many more options and combinations available. Always refer to the man pages (man find) when in doubt or when you need more specific functionality!Give some examples of how to use grepChatGPTCertainly! grep is a powerful tool for searching text patterns within files. Here are some common usage examples:Basic SearchSearch for the word "apple" in a file:bashCopy codegrep 'apple' filename.txt
Case-insensitive SearchSearch for the word "apple" irrespective of its case:bashCopy codegrep -i 'apple' filename.txt
Recursive SearchSearch for the word "apple" in all files recursively starting from the current directory:bashCopy codegrep -r 'apple' .
Search for Multiple PatternsSearch for lines containing either "apple" or "banana":bashCopy codegrep 'apple\|banana' filename.txt
Line Number of MatchDisplay the line number where the match is found:bashCopy codegrep -n 'apple' filename.txt
Count OccurrencesCount the number of lines containing the match:bashCopy codegrep -c 'apple' filename.txt
Invert MatchDisplay lines that do not match the pattern:bashCopy codegrep -v 'apple' filename.txt
Match Whole WordSearch for lines containing the whole word "apple" (not "apples" or "pineapple"):bashCopy codegrep -w 'apple' filename.txt
Search in Multiple FilesSearch for "apple" in multiple files:bashCopy codegrep 'apple' file1.txt file2.txt
Extended Regular ExpressionsUse extended regex features. For instance, to search for lines containing either "apple" or "banana" using the | operator:bashCopy codegrep -E 'apple|banana' filename.txt
Display Surrounding LinesShow 2 lines before and after each match:bashCopy codegrep -C 2 'apple' filename.txt
Search for a Pattern in a Piped OutputFor instance, to find "apple" in the list of processes:bashCopy codeps aux | grep 'apple'
These are some basic and commonly used examples. grep has many more options and capabilities, so always refer to the man pages (man grep) when in doubt or when you need more specific functionality!I think we should develop a distinctions between how computers typically store things by recording them exactly but humans store things by summarizing and paraphrasingChatGPTAbsolutely, there's a marked difference between how computers and humans store and retrieve information. Here's a distinction between the two:Computers:Literal Storage: Computers, especially traditional storage mechanisms, store data in a precise and literal manner. Every bit of data has a specific location and can be retrieved exactly as it was saved.Uniformity: Data storage and retrieval processes are consistent. Given the same input, the output or retrieval will always be the same.Volume: Computers can store vast amounts of data without any degradation in the quality or accuracy of that data.Dependency on External Systems: Computers rely on file systems, databases, and other storage mechanisms to recall information. They don't "forget" unless there's a system malfunction or the data is deliberately deleted.Lack of Abstraction: While computers can process abstractions in data (like summarizing texts using algorithms), the primary storage mode is non-abstract, holding the full and exact version of the data.Humans:Abstract Storage: Humans often store memories, experiences, and information in an abstracted manner. Rather than remembering every detail, we remember the gist, essence, or meaning.Inconsistency: Our recall of events or information can change over time. Memories can be influenced by subsequent experiences or even by the act of recalling them.Limitation: There's a limit to how much and how accurately we can remember things, especially over long periods.Emotional Influence: Our memories and understanding of information can be influenced by our emotions, beliefs, and values.Adaptive & Contextual: We often adapt our memories based on context. For instance, we might paraphrase a story differently based on who we're talking to.Loss & Degradation: Unlike computers, humans can forget information over time if not periodically revisited. Memories can degrade, and details can become fuzzy.Integration: We integrate new information with existing knowledge, often leading to a synthesized understanding or perspective.In essence, while computers store information in a static, precise, and vast manner, human memory is dynamic, abstract, and influenced by numerous internal and external factors. This distinction is crucial, especially when designing systems or tools intended to augment or interface with human memory and cognition.Now this distinction is becoming fuzzy as people are discovering sparse methods to encode information. Computers only seem to record and display things exactly because of perceptual boundaries,  conventional categorization schemes, and change blindness. Consider the following two abstracts for more context.

Philos Trans R Soc Lond B Biol Sci. 2014 Feb 19; 369(1636): 20130045.  doi: 10.1098/rstb.2013.0045
PMCID: PMC3886333PMID: 24395972
Do we have an internal model of the outside world?
Michael F. Land
Author information Copyright and License information PMC Disclaimer
Go to:
ABSTRACT
Our phenomenal world remains stationary in spite of movements of the eyes, head and body. In addition, we can point or turn to objects in the surroundings whether or not they are in the field of view. In this review, I argue that these two features of experience and behaviour are related. The ability to interact with objects we cannot see implies an internal memory model of the surroundings, available to the motor system. And, because we maintain this ability when we move around, the model must be updated, so that the locations of object memories change continuously to provide accurate directional information. The model thus contains an internal representation of both the surroundings and the motions of the head and body: in other words, a stable representation of space. Recent functional MRI studies have provided strong evidence that this egocentric representation has a location in the precuneus, on the medial surface of the superior parietal cortex. This is a region previously identified with âself-centred mental imageryâ, so it seems likely that the stable egocentric representation, required by the motor system, is also the source of our conscious percept of a stable world.

Keywords: visual stability, egocentric memory, spatial updating, precuneus

Do Large Language Models learn world models or just surface statistics?
21.JAN.2023 . 15 MIN READ
A mystery
Large Language Models (LLM) are on fire, capturing public attention by their ability to provide seemingly impressive completions to user prompts (NYT coverage). They are a delicate combination of a radically simplistic algorithm with massive amounts of data and computing power. They are trained by playing a guess-the-next-word game with itself over and over again. Each time, the model looks at a partial sentence and guesses the following word. If it makes it correctly, it will update its parameters to reinforce its confidence; otherwise, it will learn from the error and give a better guess next time.

While the underpinning training algorithm remains roughly the same, the recent increase in model and data size has brought about qualitatively new behaviors such as writing basic code or solving logic puzzles.

How do these models achieve this kind of performance? Do they merely memorize training data and reread it out loud, or are they picking up the rules of English grammar and the syntax of C language? Are they building something like an internal world modelâan understandable model of the process producing the sequences?

From various philosophical [1] and mathematical [2] perspectives, some researchers argue that it is fundamentally impossible for models trained with guess-the-next-word to learn the âmeanings'' of language and their performance is merely the result of memorizing âsurface statisticsâ, i.e., a long list of correlations that do not reflect a causal model of the process generating the sequence. Without knowing if this is the case, it becomes difficult to align the model to human values and purge spurious correlations picked up by the model [3,4]. This issue is of practical concern since relying on spurious correlations may lead to problems on out-of-distribution data.

The goal of our paper [5] is to explore this question in a carefully controlled setting. As we will discuss, we find interesting evidence that simple sequence prediction can lead to the formation of a world model. But before we dive into technical details, we start with a parable.

A thought experiment
Consider the following thought experiment. Imagine you have a friend who enjoys the board game Othello, and often comes to your house to play. The two of you take the competition seriously and are silent during the game except to call out each move as you make it, using standard Othello notation. Now imagine that there is a crow perching outside of an open window, out of view of the Othello board. After many visits from your friend, the crow starts calling out moves of its ownâand to your surprise, those moves are almost always legal given the current board.

You naturally wonder how the crow does this. Is it producing legal moves by "haphazardly stitching togetherâ [3] superficial statistics, such as which openings are common or the fact that the names of corner squares will be called out later in the game? Or is it somehow tracking and using the state of play, even though it has never seen the board? It seems like there's no way to tell.

But one day, while cleaning the windowsill where the crow sits, you notice a grid-like arrangement of two kinds of birdseed--and it looks remarkably like the configuration of the last Othello game you played. The next time your friend comes over, the two of you look at the windowsill during a game. Sure enough, the seeds show your current position, and the crow is nudging one more seed with its beak to reflect the move you just made. Then it starts looking over the seeds, paying special attention to parts of the grid that might determine the legality of the next move. Your friend, a prankster, decides to try a trick: distracting the crow and rearranging some of the seeds to a new position. When the crow looks back at the board, it cocks its head and announces a move, one that is only legal in the new, rearranged position.

At this point, it seems fair to conclude the crow is relying on more than surface statistics. It evidently has formed a model of the game it has been hearing about, one that humans can understand and even use to steer the crow's behavior. Of course, there's a lot the crow may be missing: what makes a good move, what it means to play a game, that winning makes you happy, that you once made bad moves on purpose to cheer up your friend, and so on. We make no comment on whether the crow âunderstandsâ what it hears or is in any sense âintelligentâ. We can say, however, that it has developed an interpretable (compared to in the crowâs head) and controllable (can be changed with purpose) representation of the game state.

Othello-GPT: a synthetic testbed
As a clever reader might have already guessed, the crow is our subject under debate, a large language model.

We are looking into the debate by training a GPT model only on Othello game scripts, termed Othello-GPT. Othello is played by two players (black and white), who alternatively place discs on an 8x8 board. Every move must flip more than one opponent's discs by outflanking/sandwiching them in a straight line. Game ends when no moves could be made and the player with more discs on the board wins.

We choose the game Othello, which is simpler than chess but maintains a sufficiently large game tree to avoid memorization. Our strategy is to see what, if anything, a GPT variant learns simply by observing game transcripts without any a priori knowledge of rules or board structure.


Fig 2: From left to right: the starting board state of Othello; after black places a disc at E3; after white then places a disc at D3.
Itâs worth pointing out a key difference between our model and Reinforcement Learning models like AlphaGo: to AlphaGo, game scripts are the history used to predict the optimal best next move leading to a win, so the game rule and board structures are baked into it as much as possible; in contrast, game scripts is no different from sequences with a unique generation process to Othello-GPT and to what extent the generation process can be discovered by a large language model is exactly what we are interested in. Therefore, unlike AlphaGo, no knowledge of board structure or game rules is given. The model is rather trained to learn to make legal moves only from lists of moves like: E3, D3, C4â¦ Each of the tiles is tokenized as a single word. The Othello-GPT is then trained to predict the next move given the preceding partial game to capture the distribution of games (sentences) in game datasets.

We found that the trained Othello-GPT usually makes legal moves. The error rate is 0.01%; and for comparison, the untrained Othello-GPT has an error rate of 93.29%. This is much like the observation in our parable that the crow was announcing the next moves.

Probes
To test this hypothesis, we first introduce probing, an established technique in NLP [6] to test for internal representations of information inside neural networks. We will use this technique to identify world models in a synthetic language model if they exist.

The heuristic is simple: for a classifier with constrained capacity, the more informative its input is for a certain target, the higher accuracy it can achieve when trained to predict the target. In this case, the simple classifiers are called probes, which take different activations in the model as input and are trained to predict certain properties of the input sentence, e.g., the part-of-speech tags and parse tree depth. Itâs believed that the higher accuracy these classifiers can get, the better the activations have learned about these real-world properties, i.e., the existence of these concepts in the model.

One early work [7] probed sentence embeddings with 10 linguistic properties like tense, parsing tree depth, and top constituency. Later people found that syntax trees are embedded in the contextualized word embeddings of BERT models [8].

Back to the mystery on whether large language models are learning surface statistics or world models, there have been some tantalizing clues suggesting language models may build interpretable âworld modelsâ with probing techniques. They suggest language models can develop world models for very simple concepts in their internal representations (layer-wise activations), such as color [9], direction [10], or track boolean states during synthetic tasks [11]. They found that the representations for different classes of these concepts are easier to separate compared to those from randomly-initialized models. By comparing probe accuracies from trained language models with the probe accuracies from randomly-initialized baseline, they conclude that the language models are at least picking up something about these properties.

Probing Othello-GPT
As a first step of looking into it, we apply probes to our trained Othello-GPT. For each internal representation in the model, we have a ground truth board state that it corresponds to. We then train 64 independent two-layer MLP classifiers to classify each of the 64 tiles on Othello board into three states, black, blank, and white, by taking the internal representations from Othello-GPT as input. It turns out that the error rates of these probes are reduced from 26.2% on a randomly-initialized Othello-GPT to only 1.7% on a trained Othello-GPT. This suggests that there exists a world model in the internal representation of a trained Othello-GPT. Now, what is its shape? Do these concepts organize themselves in the high-dimensional space with a geometry similar to their corresponding tiles on an Othello board?

Since the probe we trained for each tile essentially keeps its knowledge about the board with a prototype vector for that tile, we interpret it as the concept vector for that tile. For the 64 concept vectors at hand, we apply PCA to reduce the dimensionality to 3 to plot the 64 dots below, each corresponding to one tile on the Othello board. We connect two dots if the two tiles they correspond to are direct neighbors. If the connection is horizontal on board, we color it with an orange gradient palette, changing along with the vertical position of the two tiles. Similarly, we use a blue gradient palette for vertical connections. Dots for the upper left corner ([0, 0]) and lower right corner ([7, 7]) are labeled.

By contrasting with the geometry of probes trained on a randomly-initialized GPT model (left), we can confirm that the training of Othello-GPT gives rise to an emergent geometry of âdraped cloth on a ballâ (right), resembling the Othello board.


Fig 3: Left: probe geometry of a randomly-initialized Othello-GPT; right: probe geometry of a trained Othello-GPT. 
Finding these probes is like discovering the board made of seeds on the crow's windowsill. Their existence excites us but we are not yet sure if the crow is relying on them to announce the next moves.  

Controlling model predictions via uncovered world models
Remember the prank in the thought experiment? We devise a method to change the world representation of Othello-GPT by changing its intermediate activations as the neural network computes layer by layer, on the fly, in the hope that the next-step predictions of the model can be changed accordingly as if made from this new world representation. This addresses some potential criticisms that these world representations are not actually contributing to the final prediction of Othello-GPT.

The following picture shows one such intervention case: on the bottom left is the world state in the modelâs mind before the intervention, and to its right is the post-intervention world state we chose and the consequent post-intervention made by the model. What we are thinking of doing is flipping E6 from black to white and hope the model will make different next-step predictions based on the changed world state. This change in the world state will cause a change in the set of legal next moves according to the rule of Othello. If the intervention is successful, the model will change its prediction accordingly.


Fig 4: an example of the intervention experiment.
We evaluate this by comparing the ground-truth post-intervention legal moves returned by the Othello engine and those returned by the model. It turns out that it achieves an average error of only 0.12 tiles. It shows that the world representations are more than probable from the internal activations of the language model, but are also directly used for prediction. This ties back to the prank in the parable where moving the seeds around can change how the crow thinks about the game and makes the next move prediction.

A more stringent test is done by intervening the board state in the modelâs mind into ones that are unreachable from any input sequences, e.g., boards with two disconnected blocks of discs. The idea is similar to Fischer random chessâplayersâ abilities are tested by playing under impossible board states in normal chess. The systematic evaluation result is equally good, which provides evidence that further disentangles the world model from sequence statistics.ChatGPTThese abstracts present an exploration into the concept of internal world models. 6 The mental model of a text as a basis for linking information: Syntactic and semantic processes in anaphor resolution
A central part of Garnhamâs definition of mental models in text comprehension was its utility for linking different parts of a text. A linguistic means to link information are anaphoric expressions like definite noun phrases (as âthe womanâ or âthe girlâ in 5, 6, 7 above) or pronouns. A very plausible assumption would be that finding the referent of an anaphor should mostly be based on the mental model of the current discourse. And in fact, this has been proposed by various authors.

A seminal study by Gernsbacher (1991) looked at conceptual anaphora like (13 a-c). What is interesting here is that there is always a singular referent in the first sentence which is referred to by a plural pronoun in the second. In her experiments, Gernsbacher does not find any difficulty in processing the plural pronouns in sentences like these. She argues that participants consult their mental model of the situation, which is enriched by background knowledge, when they look for a referent of the pronoun. Since we know that there is more than one margarita in a bar, more than one plate in a kitchen and so forth, we can easily find the antecedent for the plural pronoun.

(13)
a.
I think Iâll order a frozen margarita. I just love them.

b.
I need a plate. Where do you keep them?

c.
My motherâs always bugging me to wear a dress. She thinks I look good in them.


However, the story may be a little more complicated than that. The plural pronoun here is only viable if the entity introduced in the first sentence allows for a non-specific, general, and/or collective reading. See for example (14a,b), where the singular pronoun in (14a) enforces the specific reading whereas the indefinite noun phrase âa Japanese womanâ has to be interpreted as non-specific with the plural pronoun in (14b).

(14)
Chris wants to marry a Japanese woman.
a.
She is just the kind of woman he likes.

b.
They are just the kind of women he likes.


What makes the so-called conceptual anaphor viable here is not the knowledge that there is more than one Japanese woman in the world, but that (14b) has a strong non-specific reading. What we need again, is a semantic level with constraints over specific and non-specific interpretations of noun-phrases that surely interacts with the mental model of the current discourse.

There is additional evidence that early processes in pronoun resolution are mostly based on lower level (mostly syntactic) principles. Sturt (2003) showed that only antecedents which are available according to syntactic binding constraints are considered in very early stages of processing, whereas all entities available in the mental model of the current discourse can be taken into account at later stages.

Another way to look at the kinds of processes involved in early anaphor resolution is the analysis of EEG-patterns while participants are listening to or reading sentences involving anaphoric violations. It has been proposed that different types of potential shifts directly following a linguistic event (event related potentials, anaphor resolutions) correlate with different types of linguistic processes (see Friederici 1999, for a full model based on different components). A positive shift peaking at about 600 msec after the event (P600) is often found following syntactic violations (e.g. agreement violations as in âthe girl sing;â Hagoort & Brown 1994). A negative shift peaking at about 400 msec (N400) has been established for semantic integration violations (e.g. as in âthe boy kicked the milk,â Kutas & Hillyard 1980). E.g., knowing there's typically more than one margarita in a bar helps readers connect "them" to "a frozen margarita."Specific vs. Non-specific Interpretations:The plural pronoun's viability hinges on the general or non-specific interpretation of the entity mentioned earlier. For instance, in (14), "a Japanese woman" can either refer to a specific individual or Japanese women in general, depending on the subsequent use of a singular or plural pronoun.There's a need for a semantic layer that determines specific vs. non-specific interpretations, which interacts with the current mental model of the discourse.Early Processes in Anaphor Resolution:Sturt's 2003 findings indicate that during the initial stages of processing, only antecedents fitting syntactic binding rules are considered. It underscores the complexity of language processing and how different cognitive and neurological factors contribute to text comprehension.
Anaphora
Anaphora can be defined as a relation between two linguistic elements, in which the interpretation of one (called an anaphor) is in some way determined by the interpretation of the other (called an antecedent).

From: International Encyclopedia of the Social & Behavioral Sciences, 2001

Related terms:
PsycholinguisticsMental ModelPronounSentence ProcessingDiscourse ReferentMetaphorsConstrualPrepositional Phrase

Anaphora
Y. Huang, in International Encyclopedia of the Social & Behavioral Sciences, 2001

1.2 Anaphora and Truth-conditions
From a truth-conditional, semantic point of view, anaphora can be divided into five types: (a) referential anaphora, one that refers to some entity in the external world either directly or via its co-reference with its antecedent in the same sentence/discourse (1); (b) bound-variable anaphora, which is interpretable by virtue of its dependency on some quantificational expression in the same sentence/discourse, thus seeming to be the natural language counterpart of a bound variable in first-order logic (8); (c) E[vans]-type anaphora, one which, for technical reasons, is neither a pure referential anaphor nor a pure bound-variable anaphor, but which nevertheless constitutes a unified semantic type of its own (9); (d) anaphora of âlaziness,â so-called because it is neither a referential anaphor nor a bound-variable anaphor, but functions as a shorthand for a repetition of its antecedent (10); and (e) bridging cross-reference anaphora, one that is used to establish a link of association with some preceding expression in the same sentence/discourse via the addition of background assumption (11) (see Huang 2000a and references therein).

(8) Every little girl wishes that she could visit the land of Lilliput.

(9) Most people who bought a donkey have treated it well.

(10) The man who gave his paycheck to his wife was wiser than the man who gave it to his mistress.

(11) John walked into a library. The music reading room had just been refurbishedChatGPTThis extract from the International Encyclopedia of the Social & Behavioral Sciences (2001) by Y. Huang provides a detailed categorization of anaphora from a truth-conditional, semantic perspective.Let's distill the key points:Definition of Anaphora:Anaphora refers to the relationship between two linguistic elements where one (the anaphor) gains its interpretation from the other (the antecedent).Types of Anaphora:Referential Anaphora:Refers to an entity in the external world directly or through co-reference with its antecedent within the same discourse. Example not provided in the extract, but a basic illustration could be: "John went to the park. He had a great time."Bound-Variable Anaphora:This type depends on a quantificational expression within the same sentence or discourse, resembling a bound variable in first-order logic.Example: (8) Every little girl wishes that she could visit the land of Lilliput. Here, "she" refers to "Every little girl".E-type Anaphora:Neither purely referential nor a pure bound-variable anaphor, but it's a unique semantic category.Example: (9) Most people who bought a donkey have treated it well. Here, "it" refers to the "donkey" bought by "most people".Anaphora of âLazinessâ:It acts as a shorthand for its antecedent's repetition rather than being a referential or bound-variable anaphor.Example: (10) The man who gave his paycheck to his wife was wiser than the man who gave it to his mistress. Here, "it" stands in for "his paycheck".Bridging Cross-Reference Anaphora:Establishes a connection with a preceding expression in the same discourse by adding a background assumption.Example: (11) John walked into a library. The music reading room had just been refurbished. "The music reading room" is associated with "library" even though it's not an explicit antecedent.These categories illuminate the varied nature of anaphoric references and underscore the intricate ways in which language conveys meaning by relying on prior information within a discourse.Space, Time, and Story
Barbara Tversky, ... Julie Morrison, in Psychology of Learning and Motivation, 2013

Abstract
Life presents as a continuous multimodal barrage on all our senses. From that, we abstract events, discrete units characterized by completion of goals and peaks of action. Effective communication of sequences of events in explanations and narratives is similarly segmented, and linked globally by overall themes and locally by anaphora. Visuospatial explanations and narratives, notably diagrams, comics, and gestures, rely on congruity of mappings of elements and relations of ideas to space and marks in space. Just as we design visuospatial discourse, we design the world: Our design actions in space create diagrams in the world, patterns, piles, rows, one-to-one correspondences, and the like, that express abstractions, categories, hierarchies, dimensions, and more, a circular process termed spraction.ChatGPTThe given abstract from "Space, Time, and Story" by Barbara Tversky, Julie Morrison, and colleagues (2013) in the "Psychology of Learning and Motivation" discusses the cognitive processing and representation of life experiences. Here's a breakdown of the main points presented:Continuous Sensory Input:Our senses are constantly bombarded with a multimodal array of information from the world around us.Abstraction of Events:From this continuous stream of information, humans derive or abstract "events," which are understood as discrete units marked by the completion of specific goals or peak moments of action.Communication of Sequences:Effective communication, whether it's explanations or narratives, also organizes sequences of events in a segmented manner.This segmentation is connected by overarching themes globally and by anaphora (referential linguistic expressions) locally.Visuospatial Communication:Modes of communication such as diagrams, comics, and gestures are visual/spatial in nature.They communicate effectively by establishing congruent mappings between ideas and their spatial/visual representation. This means that the spatial arrangement and design of elements in these forms of communication reflect the relationships and properties of the ideas they represent.Design Actions in Space:Humans don't just interpret the world; we actively design it.Our actions create patterns, arrangements, and other visual cues in the environment, which can be seen as real-world diagrams.These arrangements (like piles, rows, or patterns) can represent more abstract concepts like categories, hierarchies, and other relationships.Spraction:The abstract introduces a term, "spraction," which seems to refer to the cyclical process where our understanding of abstract concepts influences our spatial actions, which in turn reinforce or express our abstract understanding.In essence, the abstract emphasizes the interconnectedness of spatial, temporal, and narrative comprehension and representation in our cognition.Give a kind of modular breakdown of the spraction cycleChatGPTCertainly! The "spraction" cycle, as mentioned in the abstract, refers to the cyclical process where our understanding of abstract concepts influences our spatial actions, which in turn reinforce or express our abstract understanding. Let's break it down into a modular representation:1. Sensory Perception:Initial input from the environment is received through our senses, presenting a continuous stream of information.2. Cognitive Abstraction:The brain abstracts "events" from this sensory stream. These events are characterized by goal completions or action peaks.This involves identifying patterns, relationships, and significant moments in the continuous flow of experiences.3. Formation of Abstract Concepts:Derived from the abstraction of events, the brain forms abstract ideas or concepts.This includes categories, relationships, hierarchies, and other higher-order cognitive structures.4. Design Intent:Based on the abstract concepts formed, there's an intention to represent, express, or act upon these ideas in the real world.This can involve planning, decision-making, or problem-solving processes.5. Spatial Actions (Spraction):These abstract concepts drive specific actions in space.Humans design or modify their environment based on their conceptual understanding. This includes creating patterns, alignments, arrangements, or other spatial constructs that serve as real-world representations of abstract concepts.6. Environmental Feedback:Once the environment is modified or designed, it provides a new set of sensory feedback.These spatial constructs, now present in the environment, serve as external cues or diagrams that reflect and reinforce the original abstract concepts.7. Reinforcement of Abstract Concepts:By observing and interacting with the designed environment, the initial abstract concepts are reinforced, refined, or sometimes re-evaluated.8. Cycle Repeats:The reinforced abstract concepts can lead to further spatial actions or modifications, and the cycle continues.This modular breakdown showcases the dynamic interplay between cognitive abstractions and real-world actions, emphasizing the continuous feedback loop that exists between understanding concepts and designing or interacting with our environment.I'll get back to that in a second.

Inferences in Discourse, Psychology of
W. Vonk, L.G.M. Noordman, in International Encyclopedia of the Social & Behavioral Sciences, 2001

3 Some Established Findings in Inference Research
The issue that has stimulated much psycholinguistic research on inferences since the early 1970s is the following question: Which inferences are made and which ones are not made? This issue was investigated by looking for classifications of inferences, on the assumption that some kinds of inference are made during reading and some other kinds are not.

Most common is the distinction between necessary and elaborative inferences. Inferences are called necessary if without them the text representation is not coherent. Inferences that are not necessary for coherence are called elaborative, optional, or embellishing. Two aspects of coherence can be distinguished. Referential coherence is achieved by the fact that a sentence in a text deals with entities that are expressed earlier in the text. Linguistic devices to indicate referential coherence are referential (or anaphoric) expressions, such as pronouns and definite noun phrases that refer to an antecedent in the text (see Anaphora Resolution). Relational coherence is achieved by the fact that the content of a sentence has a conceptual relation with other sentences, such as a causal, contrastive, or concessive relation. Relational coherence can be expressed by conjunctions. If, in text (a), the inference about the co-referentiality between âitâ and âpartyâ or the inference about causality between âvotingâ and âshiftâ is not made, there is no coherence between the sentences. The pronoun âitâ is considered as an instruction to find a particular referent; the conjunction âbecauseâ is considered as an instruction to find a causal relation in the context. If such instructions are not executed, the representation is not coherent. In this way, inferences triggered by referential expressions and conjunctions contribute to the coherence of the representation.

The notion of necessary inference is sometimes defined in a different way and contrasted with pragmatic inference. An inference is necessary if it follows logically from the propositions in a text. From âJohn is taller than Pete and Pete is taller than Billâ, it follows that John is taller than Bill. The causal inference between the propositions with âvotingâ and âshiftâ in (a) is not only necessary for the coherence, but also necessarily follows from the text. It can be deduced from the conjunction âbecauseâ and is called a conventional implicature.

Inferences that do not follow logically from the text are called pragmatic inferences.

Another distinction that is made frequently is that between backward and forward inferences. Backward inferences relate the current part of the text to an earlier part. Forward inferences anticipate information that might be expressed in the subsequent text. Backward inferences contribute in general to the coherence of the text and are in that sense necessary inferences, while forward inferences are not. Backward inferences are much more restricted than forward inferences: the reader can anticipate a great number of things.

Inferences can also be classified with respect to their content. This yields a list of inferences that can be extended ad libitum. It includes inferences about instruments, causes, consequences, goals, time, place, and protagonists.ChatGPTThe passage you provided delves into the psychology of inferences made during discourse. Introduction: Life, Perception, and Stories
There is life. There is the perception of life. There are stories of life.

Life just keeps happening: unbroken, continually, continuously, ubiquitously, and inexorably. Life happens in sight, in sound, in smell, and in touch: all at once and from all directions. Life is outside. It happens in space and time. It happens without narration and without explanation.

The analyses of diagrams and gestures have led to a collection of features that 
characterize them and differentiate them, on the one hand from the things they represent 
and on the other hand from words and sentences. Like things are grouped by proximity 
and spatially separated from unlike things. Things are lined up meaningfully in rows 
and columns, and the rows and columns are sometimes ordered. Together, the rows and 
columns create tables or matrices, organized by the features of the things enclosed in 
the tables. The groups and orders are sometimes emphasized with lines and frames. In 
diagrams, these features create patterns that are regular, good Gestalts, and differ from 
the natural patterns in the world; they catch the eye. They invite explanations for the 
organization. Why are things grouped and ordered in those ways? This kind of 
reasoning is part of the âworkâ of diagrams, some of the ways that diagrams promote 
inference and discovery. 
The designed world uses those same features to the same ends, gathering like things, 
separating them from unlike things, arranging things in meaningful orders, and more. 
Dishes, flatware, pots and pans are grouped into drawers and shelves of like things, that 
is, categories. They are further grouped into hierarchies of categories, plates on one 
shelf, bowls on another, both grouped by size. Books are grouped and ordered on 
shelves, perhaps by topic or size or time. These visual-spatial organizations express 
taxonomic categories of kinds and hierarchies of categories. The spaces designed by 
people reflect partonomic thematic organizations as well. The kitchen contains the 
things used for cooking and eating. The bathroom encloses the things used for personal 
care. Table settings express one-to-one correspondences. Assembly lines order in time 
the object-action couplings that form events, putting on clothes or preparing a meal or 
manufacturing automobiles (see Kirsh 1995). These visual and spatial arrangements 
that people impose on the world articulate conceptual correspondences, abstractions. 
Like diagrams, they create patterns that invite the mind to discover their meanings. 
The spatial structures that cultures impose on their environments, inside and out, 
express more: they both affect and reflect the social, political, and economic 
Characterizing Diagrams

organizations of those societies. The spatial arrangements of the remnants of ancient 
cultures are used by archeologists and historians as clues to the social, political, and 
economic organization of those societies. Is there division of activities within house- 
holds, across households? Is size of space equal across family units or are some families 
allocated more space than others? What structures are proximal, which ones distant? 
Spatial size, proximity, grouping, framing with furnishings and walls all carry social- 
political-economic meaning. People use those visual and spatial arrangements to make 
inferences from the spatial to the social-political-economic in their daily lives. Spatial 
relations suggest social relations: people who sit or walk close to each other are more 
likely to be seen as a social group, and their degree of spatial closeness to reflect their 
social closeness. 
The patterns in space that articulate abstractions are created by actions in space. 
Those actions are incorporated into gestures used to express acts of thought, 
putting, taking, lifting, sorting, pushing, separating, piling, and more. Those 
patterns are used to create the diagrams on paper that have more deliberate intent 
to communicate abstractions. Actions in space express abstractions and create 
abstractions, interlinked processes that have been dubbed spraction (e. g., Tversky 
2011a, 2011b). Forming categories of kinds, parts, and events in the mind helps to 
simplify and organize Jamesâ proverbial âblooming buzzing confusionâ of sensory 
input. Similarly, designing the world to reflect kinds, parts, events, and other 
abstractions simplifies a complex world by integrating and organizing both per- 
ception and action. The simple patterns created by design actions form good 
gestalts that attract the eye and inform and stimulate the mind. Those patterns 
are used to create the diagrams on paper that have more deliberate intent to 
communicate. The patterns in space are designed by action and support action, 
performing the basic tasks of life, navigation inside and outside, eating, sleeping, 
work, and play. Action. Space. Spraction. Inseparable and integrated.

**The Anaphoric Nature of Perception and Cognition: Bridging Linguistics and Action**

In our quest to understand the intricacies of the human mind, two seemingly disparate concepts emerge: anaphora resolution in linguistics and spraction as described by Barbara Tversky. However, upon closer examination, these notions converge, revealing the profound ways in which humans perceive, process, and represent information.

**Anaphora and Intentionality**

Anaphora resolution is a cognitive task rooted in linguistics, dealing with the linkage of pronouns or referential expressions to their respective antecedents. Take the sentence "Jane went to the store because she needed groceries." Here, "she" acts as an anaphoric reference to "Jane." This process is emblematic of the brain's innate ability to establish continuity and coherence in narratives, reducing redundancy and facilitating comprehension.

Beyond linguistics, we've broadened the term "anaphora" to encompass intentionality or a directed goal state. In this light, both humans and artificial systems act as anaphoric agents, drawing on past experiences or data to shape their future actions. Such a framework suggests that the principle underpinning anaphora resolution is not just linguistic but pervasive across various cognitive tasks.

**Spraction: The Spatial Cognition of Abstract Thought**

Barbara Tversky's concept of spraction illuminates the link between actions in space and abstract thought. Humans often externalize cognitive processes, translating intangible ideas into physical, spatial manifestations. Think of a teacher using blocks to represent mathematical concepts or a mind map capturing the interconnectedness of ideas. Spraction is about this translation, highlighting the human propensity to simplify and structure the environment for more effective understanding.

**Connecting the Dots: Anaphora and Spraction**

1. **Cognitive Linking**: Both anaphora resolution and spraction pivot around cognitive linking. While anaphora resolution connects pronouns to antecedents, spraction links spatial actions to abstract notions. This linkage facilitates more effortless navigation of information, be it in linguistic or spatial realms.

2. **Organizing Information**: Humans innately seek patterns and structures. Anaphora resolution and spraction serve this end, organizing linguistic and spatial data respectively, crafting coherent narratives or representations.

3. **Externalization**: Both processes serve as tools to externalize thought. Anaphora resolution allows for succinct communication by forgoing the need for repetition, whereas spraction manifests abstract thought in tangible, physical forms.

4. **Facilitating Understanding**: At their core, both anaphora resolution and spraction aim to simplify complexity. They act as bridges, aiding humans in grasping intricate linguistic narratives or understanding abstract ideas through spatial representation.

5. **Mirroring Thought Processes**: These concepts not only aid understanding but also offer windows into cognitive processes. Just as anaphora resolution unveils the cognitive linkage of referential expressions, spraction provides insights into how spatial actions correlate with abstract reasoning.

**Conclusion**

In essence, anaphora resolution and spraction epitomize the human endeavor to interpret and structure the world. They underscore the multifaceted ways in which our brains process information, be it through the prism of language or the lens of spatial action. These cognitive tools not only shape our understanding but also reflect the intricate ballet of neural processes that underpin human cognition. By exploring these parallels, we gain a deeper appreciation of the marvel that is the human mind.ChatGPTThe Anaphoric Nature of Perception and Cognition: Bridging Linguistics and ActionHuman cognition, as intricate as it is, often navigates the vast seas of information through sophisticated mechanisms. This interdisciplinary approach not only enhances our comprehension of language's intricate structures but also underscores the universality of mathematical frameworks across diverse domains.Include another example based on this: Here are the key points about sparse and redundant representations for image denoising via dictionary learning:

- The approach is based on representing image patches sparsely over a trained dictionary, rather than a predefined transform like DCT or wavelets. 

- A "Sparseland" model assumes that each image patch can be represented as a sparse linear combination of atoms in an overcomplete (redundant) dictionary. 

- Dictionary learning algorithms like K-SVD are used to learn the dictionary directly from image examples/patches.

- Local patch-based approaches are extended to whole images by defining a global Bayesian prior that enforces sparsity over patches at all locations.

- This leads to a MAP image reconstruction formulation, whose solution is an iterative algorithm alternating between sparse coding of patches and averaging results. 

- Training can also be done directly on the corrupted image, fusing training and denoising into a single iterative process of alternation. 

- Experiments demonstrate state-of-the-art denoising performance comparable to or better than leading methods like non-local means, showing the effectiveness of the local sparse approach on patches.

So in summary, the key idea is to learn an efficient sparse representation of natural images by training an overcomplete dictionary, and use this learned prior within a global Bayesian reconstruction framework for effective denoising.ChatGPTAnaphora Analysis in a Mathematical Framework: From Linguistics to Image DenoisingDelving into the world of linguistics and cognition through mathematical and computational paradigms is both intriguing and informative. Both emphasize the importance of reducing data to its most meaningful components, whether it's the most salient linguistic feature or the most defining image patch.Training Dictionaries and World Models: In image denoising, rather than utilizing predefined transforms, algorithms like K-SVD train dictionaries directly from image patches. This mirrors the concept of world models in linguistics, where individual experiences craft unique cognitive 'dictionaries' over time.'Sparseland' and Riemann Surfaces: The 'Sparseland' model in image denoising posits that each image patch can be represented sparsely over a redundant dictionary. This mirrors the cognitive process of navigating the 'noisy' real-world information, refining understanding and beliefs as new data is encountered.In essence, both anaphora resolution in linguistics and sparse representation in image denoising hinge on the idea of filtering out noise and focusing on the most meaningful components. By drawing these parallels, we recognize the ubiquity and versatility of mathematical and computational frameworks across diverse fields.Here are more details on dictionary learning using K-SVD and the proposed denoising algorithm:

- K-SVD is an iterative algorithm that learns an overcomplete dictionary to sparsely represent a set of training samples/patches. 

- It alternates between sparse coding steps, where patches are represented over the fixed dictionary, and a dictionary update step. 

- In the dictionary update step, atoms are modified one at a time to better represent reconstruction errors of sparse representations. 

- Using a global image prior instead of just local patch sparsity enforces coherence across patches. This helps mitigate blocking artifacts.

- The MAP formulation with global prior leads to an iterative patch-by-patch sparse coding algorithm:

- In each iteration, patches are extracted from the current estimate and sparse coded over the dictionary. 

- The sparse codes are then used to reconstruct each patch, and the reconstructions are averaged and substituted back into the image.

- This has the effect of denoising while maintaining structure and coherence across patch boundaries.

- The algorithm can also iteratively update the dictionary using the noisy image, jointly optimizing denoising and representation learning in a single process.

So in summary, K-SVD learns an effective sparse dictionary, while the global priorbased iterative algorithm smoothly integrates local sparse representations to produce high quality denoised images.ChatGPTAnaphora Analysis and K-SVD in Image Denoising: A Deep Dive into Mathematical ConstructsThe intricate layers of human language, exemplified by phenomena such as anaphora, parallel the mathematical structures in image processing. The realm of dictionary learning using K-SVD for image denoising brings forth this intersection in profound ways.K-SVD as the Cognitive Schema: Much like the brain's ability to reference previously mentioned entities in language (anaphora resolution), K-SVD works iteratively, building an overcomplete dictionary tailored for sparse representation. Its two-step dance â sparse coding on a stable dictionary and then dictionary modification â mirrors the continuous cognitive adjustments we make when processing language.Atoms and Linguistic Elements: At the heart of K-SVD, atoms of the dictionary are singularly refined during the update step. They strive to minimize the reconstruction errors, akin to the precision with which antecedents in linguistics are chosen for clarity.Global Image Priors and Linguistic Context: Just as a well-defined linguistic context eases anaphora resolution, the global image prior in denoising ensures consistency across image patches, addressing common issues like blocking artifacts. Ambiguous diagrams, whether they're ephemeral gestures or tangible sketches, magnify our cognitive prowess and our innate drive to shape the abstract into the palpable.Here are the key points about how arrows convey meaning in diagrams:

- Experiments showed that arrows were interpreted as conveying causal/sequential relationships, while unlabeled diagrams were seen as depicting spatial relationships/structures.

- People given structural descriptions drew diagrams labeling parts, but not using arrows. Those given causal descriptions drew arrows showing the sequence of events but did not label parts. 

- Basic graphic elements like lines, boxes, dots and arrows form a partial semantics or vocabulary for constructing diagrams across domains. The meaning of elements changes slightly by domain.

- Diagrams have their own syntax rules for combining elements, like how lines connect to dots or boxes are arranged, and pragmatic constraints depend on the domain/type of diagram. 

- Spatial dimensions like distance, order, direction are used systematically in diagrams and mappings to represent abstract concepts like time, quantity, preferences:

- Distance represents both physical distance and abstract distances like between preferences. 

- Order and direction are systematically mapped - upwards generally represents increased/better quantities and preferences due to associations with physical strength/health. 

- These systematic mappings from space to meaning are also found in language conventions like "rising to the top" or "falling into depression."

So in summary, basic graphic elements like arrows convey systematic meanings across diagram types, and spatial dimensions systematically represent abstract concepts, showing the emergence of a shared graphical "language."ChatGPTArrows and the Semiotic Dance in Diagrams: A Treatise on Visual LanguageIn the vast tableau of human communication, diagrams, with their use of arrows, lines, dots, and boxes, stand as a testament to our innate inclination towards visual expression.
